Why Physical AI Is the Next Frontier | The a16z Show

a16z(280.0K)·80:22·4.0K views·2026-07-21
Marc AndreessenCo-founder, a16z Qasar YounisCofounder, Applied Intuition Peter LudwigCofounder, Applied Intuition
TL;DRPhysical AI is the next major economic frontier, poised to impact the global economy more deeply than digital AI by automating the trillion-dollar industries of logistics, mining, and manufacturing.
Applied Intuition’s launch of Dana, an agentic platform for physical AI,...

TL;DR

Physical AI is the next major economic frontier, poised to impact the global economy more deeply than digital AI by automating the trillion-dollar industries of logistics, mining, and manufacturing. Applied Intuition’s launch of Dana, an agentic platform for physical AI, aims to democratize the development of autonomous systems, moving from "alchemistic" engineering to a scalable "IDE for the physical world."

The Thesis of Physical AI

  • Economic Impact: While digital AI optimizes software and media, physical AI addresses the backbone of the global economy—transportation, supply chains, and manufacturing.
  • The "Billion Machines" Mission: Applied Intuition aims to put intelligence on one billion machines, ranging from passenger vehicles to defense systems and mining equipment.
  • Strategic Positioning: The company operates as a horizontal technology provider, akin to a silicon chipmaker (e.g., NVIDIA), providing "design wins" and deep integration into the hardware of various OEMs (Original Equipment Manufacturers).
      • Market Diversification: 70% of Applied Intuition’s business is currently non-automotive, focusing on sectors like defense, construction, and agriculture.
      • Engineering Heavy: Roughly 83% of the company’s 1,000+ employees are engineers, prioritizing product performance over traditional sales-led growth.

Dana: The Agentic Platform for Physical AI

  • Democratizing Autonomy: The launch of Dana represents a shift toward making autonomous system development accessible. The founders’ vision is for a high school student capable of building an iPhone app to be able to build an autonomous delivery robot or drone.
  • Agentic Interface: Dana functions as an agentic platform that collapses workflows—such as data cleaning, scenario generation, and requirements definition—from days or weeks into minutes.
  • Tooling for Humanoids: The platform is designed to lower the barrier for the "next generation" of products, specifically aiming to make humanoid robot development "real" by simplifying imitation and reinforcement learning.
  • Offboard vs. Onboard AI:
      • Offboard: Large-scale world models and simulation used for training and validation in the cloud.
      • Onboard: High-performance, real-time models that must operate within strict millisecond latencies and safety-critical constraints on the machine itself.

The Engineering of Autonomy and World Models

  • The "Sim-to-Real" Spectrum:
      • Physics-Based Simulation: Highly deterministic models that mimic material properties, reflectivity, and sensors.
      • Neural Simulation (World Models): Using generative AI to create reactive video feeds where the environment responds to the machine's actions.
  • End-to-End Reinforcement Learning: The industry is moving away from simple "imitation learning" (copying humans) toward closed-loop systems that identify their own errors and train themselves to fix them.
  • Hardware Constraints: Unlike digital AI labs that can run trillion-parameter models on massive server farms, physical AI is constrained by "on-machine" realities like overheating, sensor calibration, and battery life.

Deployment Timelines and Global Market Dynamics

  • Automotive Horizon: Qasar Younis predicts a "wait, wait, then a lot" trajectory for self-driving cars, similar to the adoption of mobile phones.
      • Prediction: Routine autonomous vehicle (AV) availability in major cities by 2028–2030, with routine ubiquity (L2++ systems being default/free) by the early 2030s.
  • Labor Shortages as a Catalyst:
      • Agriculture: The average American farmer is 58; the labor pool is shrinking while demand grows.
      • Trucking & Mining: Automation targets "dull, dirty, and dangerous" jobs. Mining accounts for 1% of global labor but 8% of work-related fatalities.
  • Sovereign AI: As AI moves into physical machines (defense/logistics), nations are demanding localized, "sovereign" AI stacks. This creates a massive opportunity for technology providers who can navigate complex geopolitical regulations.

Notable Quotes

"In this intelligence revolution, the companies that impact the physical world might actually be bigger than the companies that impact the digital world." — Qasar Younis, CEO
"Autonomy is still quite exotic in the scope of software. We want to bring that down... if I can develop a Roomba for myself in my house on a weekend using Dana, then it’s not suddenly so scary." — Qasar Younis, CEO
"The labs have it easy because they can make models that are trillions of parameters and those models can be super slow. We don’t have that luxury in physical AI. We deal in actual clock real-time." — Peter Ludwig, CTO
"No hand can block the sun. And the sun is technological progress. If we as a society don’t embrace technological progress, we will be left behind." — Qasar Younis, CEO

But when we're thinking about a world model, right, we're typically thinking about it in the context of a simulation, right? Something that is effectively... we started as a sim company. Yeah, something that is like sufficiently able to represent the real world and is reactive in a sense where you can actually have let's say an autonomous agent that's acting in this world and the world model is behaving appropriately in response to that autonomous agent. And so the more classical approaches of simulation—very physics-based. And you can decompose physics in all different ways in all different levels of abstraction and you can simulate with sensors or without sensors and is it just a body simulation or are we actually simulating for example the light in the environment or the... almost think about like the way CGI is done. Yeah, so that's sort of on the far end of physics-based simulation and then the opposite end is purely neural simulation. But within that spectrum there's many different things you can do that are each useful in their own right. And so one of those things is a Gaussian-based simulation, right, where you have a representation of the real world that has a 3D representation. And that 3D representation is consistent, meaning that if you have let's say some reference point, let's say a camera, and that camera moves within that 3D world, because the Gaussian is actually representing the 3D geometry of that world, you'll actually get very high-quality output from that. There's a lot of value in that and that's one type of world model. But when you go further on that spectrum into really into neural simulation, then you get into these where you're actually generating the video feeds. And you can think of a neural network that's actually outputting a video as what's actually coming out of the neurons of that. And that can be reactive, which gives you some very interesting properties. Reactive as in the ego does something in the environment and the other agents respond to the ego. Exactly. However, you're not guaranteed in that reactivity that it's accurate, right? And now it's a question of, well how can I align this simulation, this world model, with the real world and the way that the real world would actually react? And if you have perfect alignment between the real world and the world model, I think you've just sort of solved the universe roughly, right? It's an impossibly difficult problem. but as we make progress towards that, it makes training physical AI models much easier because you can do more of that in simulation. But the hardest part though is we're always talking about performance, right? So I like to say the labs, they have it easy because they can make models that are trillions of parameters and those models can be super slow and that's fine. But we don't have that luxury in physical AI, right? We deal in real time, like actual actual clock real time. And so we have so many milliseconds before we have to do something. And those performance constraints, they actually constrain the problem in a lot of ways. So we can have very large models and we do have very large models that are used in the offboard environment, but once you go onboard, all those constraints are very real. And now we need to train a much smaller model that has these safety constraints, these determinism constraints. And that's the hard part about physical AI. It's also the moat, right? Mark Cuban on the AI Bubble: Who Actually Gets Wiped Out?

All-In Podcast(1.1M)·41:39·26.0K views·2026-07-21
Mark CubanEntrepreneur and Investor
TL;DR"If there's a price-performance curve on AI that minimizes the power requirements, there's going to be a lot of data centers that are going to be turned into pickleball courts.
Mark Cuban argues that while AI is not a traditional dot-com retail bubble, it poses significant risks to private equity and VCs due to "pricing for perfection." He emphasizes that while enterprise AI implementation is harder than expected, the technology is a...

TL;DR: Mark Cuban argues that while AI is not a traditional dot-com retail bubble, it poses significant risks to private equity and VCs due to "pricing for perfection." He emphasizes that while enterprise AI implementation is harder than expected, the technology is a revolutionary force for entrepreneurs and a potentially objective "truth-seeker" in a polarized political landscape.

The AI Market: A "Private Capital" Bubble

  • Contrast with Dot-Com: Unlike the 1990s, today’s market isn't filled with "shit companies" going public with zero revenue; instead, the risk is concentrated in venture capital and private equity firms that deployed capital at peak valuations.
  • Infrastructure Risk: Large tech firms (Meta, Google) are borrowing hundreds of billions for CapEx and data centers.
      • The "Pickleball" Risk: If technological breakthroughs minimize power requirements or if utilization doesn't scale as expected, billions in data center investments could become obsolete.
  • The Case for Mid-Tier IPOs: Cuban urges startups to go public at $50M–$100M valuations rather than staying private.
      • Currency for M&A: Having public stock as currency allows disruptive companies to acquire legacy competitors or domain-specific data without needing expensive private cash.

The Reality of Enterprise AI Implementation

  • Implementation Friction: AI is significantly harder to integrate into large organizations than early hype suggested.
      • Manual Labor Requirement: Microsoft and OpenAI hiring thousands of "forward-deployed engineers" proves that AGI is not yet a reality; if the AI were truly "intelligent," it wouldn't require human engineers for deployment.
  • The White-Collar Job Myth: Cuban dismisses the idea that AI will wipe out 50% of white-collar jobs in the near term.
      • Limitations: Current LLMs struggle with "systems thinking" and multi-step agentic tasks. They require a "programming mindset" to iterate past failures.
  • The Productivity Gap: The divide between "AI-first" employees and those resistant to the tech is becoming as stark as the gap between PC users and legal-pad users in the 1980s.

The Entrepreneurial Revolution & Tooling

  • Speed to Market: Cuban notes that AI reduces the "blocking and tackling" of starting a business (business plans, patents, bill of materials) from months to minutes.
  • LLMs vs. World Models: Cuban highlights the shift toward "World Models" (e.g., Yann LeCun’s AMI).
      • Beyond Text: Current AI is limited by its reliance on text and images. The future involves video and sensor data to understand physical cause-and-effect (the "sippy cup" analogy).
  • Tool Hopping: Users are rapidly moving between platforms (OpenAI to Claude to Lovable) as agents become "brittle" or hallucinate. Cuban specifically highlights Lovable for its ability to generate high volumes of functional applications for non-engineers.

Healthcare, Politics, and "Truth Seeking"

  • Self-Directed Healthcare: AI enables "patient-led" medicine by analyzing personal trends in blood work and biometrics (via services like Open Evidence and wearables).
      • Doctor Synergy: AI won't replace doctors but will eliminate the "guessing" in the 95% of medicine that relies on data memorization, allowing doctors to focus on empathy and visual diagnosis.
  • The Political "Truth" Engine: Unlike social media algorithms that prioritize engagement and division, LLMs must prioritize "truth-seeking" to maintain user trust.
      • Fact-Checking Leaders: Cuban uses models like Grok and Claude to objectively vet claims made by politicians on both sides of the aisle.
  • Immigration as Talent Recruitment: Cuban stresses the economic necessity of legal immigration, likening it to a sports team's need to recruit global talent (e.g., Dirk Nowitzki or Victor Wembanyama) to remain competitive.

NBA Economics & Parity

  • Valuation Shift: NBA team values are no longer driven solely by attendance or ratings, but by their ability to drive streaming service subscriptions (e.g., Peacock, Amazon, ESPN+).
  • The "Second Apron" Effect: New salary cap rules make "three-peats" nearly impossible. Teams are forced to break up talent cores, creating unprecedented parity across the league.

***

Notable Quotes

"If there's a price-performance curve on AI that minimizes the power requirements, there's going to be a lot of data centers that are going to be turned into pickleball courts." — Mark Cuban
"Every single motherf*cking business plan ever written in the history of business plans is wrong... so what if the AI is wrong? It gives you a direction to iterate." — Mark Cuban
"Social media's currency is keeping you engaged. Large language models' currency is getting you the correct answer. It’s two different missions." — Mark Cuban
"I’m trying to tell my portfolio companies: go public, motherf*ckers, go public. You want that stock as currency to buy the competition." — Mark Cuban

Because back then, there were shit companies going public, getting crazy valuations, and people were buying them, and the stock would go up, you know, 50%, 100% with companies that had no revenue, no traffic, no nothing. And you'd go get a cab back then and people would be talking about them. You don't see that at all today. So it's not a bubble that's going to impact most people in the room, right? The 2028 Natural Gas Crisis No One Sees Coming

Invest Like The Best(82.8K)·59:45·4.5K views·2026-07-21
Matt SmithLead Researcher
TL;DRThe United States is approaching a historic, structural natural gas deficit beginning in 2028 as surging demand from LNG exports and AI data centers outpaces the nation's production and infrastructure capacity, threatening to drive electricity prices to "unbounded" heights.

TL;DR

The United States is approaching a historic, structural natural gas deficit beginning in 2028 as surging demand from LNG exports and AI data centers outpaces the nation's production and infrastructure capacity, threatening to drive electricity prices to "unbounded" heights.

The Drivers of the Structural Deficit

  • LNG Export Explosion: The U.S. currently exports approximately 15 billion cubic feet per day (Bcf/d), roughly 12–15% of daily production. This is slated to grow to 35 Bcf/d by 2030 due to long-term contracts and nearly completed infrastructure projects.
  • The AI Compute Multiplier: Beyond the "die-cast" LNG demand, AI data centers are projected to add a base case of 5 Bcf/d of incremental gas demand by the early 2030s.
      • Extreme Scenarios: If all proposed AI projects proceed (the "P0" case), demand could surge by 12–15 Bcf/d.
      • Efficiency vs. Demand: Every major AI power solution—from GE Vernova combined cycle plants to Bloom Energy fuel cells—relies on increased natural gas consumption.
  • Historical Context: Since 2010, the U.S. has enjoyed an era of "shale abundance" that lulled the market into complacency. The market is currently pricing natural gas as if this abundance is permanent, ignoring the approaching intersection of supply and demand.

Supply and Infrastructure Bottlenecks

  • Production Plateaus: While gas remains in the ground, the "flow rate" is constrained. Matt Smith estimates U.S. production can only grow by approximately 20 Bcf/d (reaching a ceiling of 128–132 Bcf/d) based on known well performance and acreage.
  • Depleting "Core" Inventory: Major producers are advanced in depleting their highest-quality acreage. Replacing this production is becoming increasingly difficult and costly.
  • Surface-Level Constraints:
      • Processing & Gathering: Massive investment is required immediately in processing plants (to remove liquids/impurities) and gathering lines to move gas from wellheads to mainlines.
      • Interstate Pipelines: Building new large-scale pipelines has become a "nightmare" due to regulatory hurdles. Only one major project (Mountain Valley Pipeline) has been completed in the last decade.
  • The Permian Myth: Investors often assume the Permian Basin (Texas) can solve any shortage. However, gas there is a byproduct of oil; producing more gas requires higher oil prices and significant new pipeline capacity, which is already largely accounted for in base models.

The 2028 "Knife Fight" and Storage Crisis

  • Storage Depletion: U.S. working gas storage (roughly 4 Tcf) typically fluctuates seasonally. Matt Smith’s modeling shows storage breaking below all historical norms by mid-2028.
  • Price Convexity: By 2029, storage levels could drop to a point where the upside risk to gas prices becomes "unbounded." Unlike previous price spikes (e.g., Winter Storm Uri), this would be a structural, multi-year shortage rather than a transitory event.
  • Inelastic Demand: Because LNG exports are governed by rigid international contracts and AI data centers require 24/7 "baseload" power, there are few levers to reduce demand quickly when the shortage hits.

Market Winners and Losers

  • Winner: Strategic Upstream Producers:
      • Expand Energy (EXE): Controls an estimated 70% of the remaining core Haynesville wells.
      • Range Resources (RRC): Positioned with high-quality inventory in Appalachia.
  • Winner: Nuclear and Utility Solar:
      • Nuclear: Seen as the only long-term "bridge" out of the crisis. Key beneficiaries include Cameco (CCJ) and BWXT.
      • Solar: Utility-scale solar assets and residential solar companies benefit as the "marginal cost" of electricity (set by gas) skyrockets while the cost of sunlight remains zero.
  • Loser: The US Consumer: Likely to face significantly higher utility bills as natural gas remains the marginal fuel for 40% of U.S. power generation.
  • Loser: Hyperscalers & Manufacturers:
      • Hyperscalers: Energy could move from 10% of compute costs to 30% or more by 2029.
      • Distributed Generation: Companies like Caterpillar (CAT) and Bloom Energy (BE) may see orders slow if the fuel required to run their turbines and fuel cells becomes prohibitively expensive or physically unavailable.

Strategic Recommendations

  • Nuclear Acceleration: The U.S. government and hyperscalers must align to build "large-scale" nuclear reactors (like the Westinghouse AP1000) to bring 10–20 gigawatts online by the mid-2030s.
  • Residential Protections: Encouraging residential solar and battery storage is a critical hedge for consumers to protect against peak power price volatility.
  • Securing Physical Supply: CEOs of energy-intensive businesses must move beyond financial hedging and secure physical supply certainty for 2028 and beyond.

***

Residential solar assets are really one of the only ways to protect yourself from what's going to happen during the time of 10 AM to 6 PM once gas gets really tight in the electricity markets, what you pay for electricity at your house. We think residential solar grows exponentially from here, even without tax incentives. For the first time it's very economic with where electricity prices are likely to go to install solar, especially when it accompanies batteries, which make the electricity much more available around the clock. - I think it's important to say you're an investor, like you have money behind this work. Beyond those two categories, are there any other like surprising winners do you think in all this? Like what about nuclear? What about Westinghouse or places like this? - Well, what we're talking about is a complex dynamic system where there will be choices to consume electricity or not at different times. And as I go through this, I want to make sure I acknowledge that there's no silver bullet solution for what I described as a convex situation with natural gas and therefore electricity prices as the decade closes. And there's no bridge fuel other than solar and wind because currently natural gas is the only flex fuel to get us to when we can bring on nuclear. We've spent a fair amount of time as well in the nuclear ecosystem. And to us, large-scale nukes are the only solution that makes sense. Which points us primarily to the AP1000 Westinghouse units that have... - Those do take like five years to build or something? - More than that. But at least they have a story passed and very brief history. We've built two nuclear reactor units in 30 years in the US: Vogtle 3 and 4. Around that time we also tried to build one in South Carolina called V.C. Summer, another nuclear project. Nearly bankrupted SCANA, which was later pushed into the arms and the project was shut down. The muscle memory from trying to build large-scale nuke, especially on the back of Fukushima in 2011, Chernobyl, Three Mile Island, for three decades nuclear engineers and scientists and companies have gone into other stuff. And then the Vogtle 3 and 4 experiment where it costs three times as much and took 15 years, I think, from birth to commercial service. That's the recent memory of building these nuclear units. But if you go out to the 2030s, what I'm describing in terms of gas deficit only gets worse in '31, '32 and beyond. And so in our mind, the only viable solution is to build large-scale nuclear as fast as possible, which would mean it needs to come on in 2033 or 2034, which is as soon as it can come on. Regulated utilities, hyperscalers, regulators should all align around that goal. But because people don't really believe that gas is in short supply as the decade goes along, they don't believe in the problem, they don't like the solution. They need to be convinced to the problem. And the country has a history of building pipelines to solve problems that exist today, not problems that will exist in five or 10 years. And so we're trying to get out ahead and see where the puck's going. And where it's going is we're going to need large-scale nuclear by 2033, 2034. - You don't think SMRs can be a solution where you use smaller reactors to power individual data centers behind the meter and this just never touches the system? - Many of the SMRs are still science experiments. The NRC and the US government are actually doing a fair number of things to break down the barriers to bringing those to fruition to see if they work or not and what the cost will be and whether they can be scaled or not. But many of these SMR companies are not set up to manufacture and truly scale for the solution that's needed for tens of gigawatts as you go into the 2030s. And so that points us to these AP1000s. Since Vogtle 4 came on, and Vogtle 4 experienced very material improvements from Vogtle 3 in Georgia. Today China is building 39 plus or minus nuclear reactors. 34 of them are 1 gigawatt plus. I think a third of those are modeled after the AP1000. We know a lot more today about building large-scale nukes than we did when these mistakes were made. Large scale to us where it can be commercialized on a known timeline and where the costs are probably better than where we don't even know if we can scale the businesses yet in terms of SMRs. Large scale versus small probably wins in our mind. Two companies most levered to that would be Cameco which owns 49%, Brookfield 51%. You'll probably find that the US government, I think, agrees with what I'm describing. They seem to really be lining up and trying to facilitate commitments on early procurement, which will de-risk some of the supply chain, which will help put timelines on this. You know, when the Westinghouse comes public and it's deeply undervalued within Cameco today, so that's an interesting one. BWXT, which is a super interesting company with they're the primary supplier of nuclear for the US Navy, they significantly benefit from the coming nuclear cycle as well, and lots of dollar content in the AP1000s. - Who are the big losers, you think, in this future? - Well, sadly, and the biggest losers of this will be the US consumer. To the point where you take what I'm saying and if we're even partially right, electricity prices rise, which you can see some of on the forward curves in these different markets. We're not anti-AI. But it consumes a lot of power. We need to really focus on the 2030 to 2035 period. The US consumer is probably going to pay the bill in the meantime. Please note that most of the solutions being proposed by the government are to consume more gas because everybody believes it's plentiful. Well, that means more gas, not less. And so every time you read a press release from Bloom or from GE Vernova, think more gas. Another loser, I want to be respectful, some of the biggest winners so far at least in the stock market have been the manufacturers of gas turbines or distributed power gen sets. Those are sort of two manufacturers of distributed generation or BTM generation that we think are probably more poorly positioned than investors appreciate. And then really doesn't make sense to us beyond 2028, 2029, 2030 to build large-scale natural gas generation until we ramp up production meaningfully and you can make sure we have the security of deliverability of supply of gas that's consistent with our model. And so we could see orders slow very meaningfully for natural gas generating assets, even on a large scale, as 2026 progresses and those could be some of the losers. Is it data center power requirements are much lower because we make performance breakthroughs or AI demand isn't what we think it's going to be, like what is this most sensitive to such that it might be wrong? - So after we did most of our work, we went on a bit of a listening tour to target conversations with who we think are maybe the subject matter experts in that thing. So energy storage or hyperscale compute deployment and energy consumption. The common pushbacks, which we've spent a lot of time understanding, are Permian oil play. Oil's high. Permian has lots of gas in the ground associated with it. Why can't Permian productivity just fix problem? So our base case model already accounts for the 7-plus Bcf a day of pipelines that are already being built or developed that come on between '26 and '30 in the Permian. If there were a new gas pipeline that would come on between now and 2030, we would know about it because of the regulatory processes and the way, the time it takes to build these pipes. And so beyond the deliverability of the resource or the gas in the ground itself, how much can actually get to market and either leave via LNG export terminals or be consumed in Texas or nearby—we've already included that in our base case model. And so, but that will be one of the pushbacks: is there plenty of gas in the Permian? But I would posit—this may be controversial—there was plenty of oil in the world before the Iran conflict surfaced. Eventually there will be oil a plenty again, that's why it was at $55 a barrel before the Iran conflict. In order to make more Permian natural gas, you also have to be incentivized to make more Permian oil. And those incentives didn't exist until Iran. In order to produce a lot more Permian gas than even these 7-plus Bcf of pipelines that we're already modeling, you need much, much higher oil for longer, which only exacerbates this consumer crisis that we're concerned about. So we don't think the Permian solves the problem. And then the other one is, well, you can locate a bunch of behind-the-meter local Permian power generation, which is what's happening. But we are modeling what has been announced and proposed. And if it's going to consume local Permian gas, that means it's not going to make it into the pipeline downstream. Nvidia Rolls Out New Chips, WBD Deal In Limbo | Bloomberg Tech 7/21/2026

Bloomberg Tech(732.0K)·49:08·1.0K views·2026-07-21
Ed LudlowAnchor, Bloomberg Tech Sameer DholakiaPartner, Bessemer Venture Partners Ian KingReporter, Bloomberg News
TL;DRNvidia has officially moved its next-generation "Vera Rubin" AI platform into full production to quell market skepticism, while a federal judge’s temporary restraining order has thrown the Paramount-Warner Bros.
Discovery merger into a high-stakes legal limbo. Vera Rubin...

TL;DR

Nvidia has officially moved its next-generation "Vera Rubin" AI platform into full production to quell market skepticism, while a federal judge’s temporary restraining order has thrown the Paramount-Warner Bros. Discovery merger into a high-stakes legal limbo.

Nvidia and the Semiconductor Rebound

  • Vera Rubin Rollout: Nvidia has confirmed that its next-generation AI chip platform, Vera Rubin, is in full production and already shipping to major customers.
      • Efficiency Gains: According to Ian King (Bloomberg News), new server racks (NV72) are now being assembled by robots to eliminate human error, reducing data center deployment time from hours to "tens of minutes."
      • Market Sentiment: The Philadelphia Semiconductor Index (SOX) surged over 4% following reports that Nvidia is "slaying the dragon" of rumors regarding production delays or material issues.
  • TSMC Pricing Pressure: Reports indicate TSMC plans to raise chipmaking prices by up to 10% starting in 2027.
      • Strategic Rationale: Peter Elstrom (Bloomberg) notes this is an effort to capture more profit margin from high-margin clients like Nvidia and Apple as TSMC expands manufacturing into the U.S.
  • Memory Sector Warning: JoAnne Feeney (Advisors Capital Partners) cautioned that the memory sector, specifically Micron, remains highly cyclical. She warned that current high prices are unsustainable as competitors collectively add too much capacity.

The AI Race: China, IP, and Hyper-Growth

  • The "Kimi" Factor: Moonshot AI’s release of the Kimi K3 model (2.8 trillion parameters) has sparked a re-evaluation of AI economics.
      • Distillation Concerns: Washington is increasing scrutiny on Chinese models for "distillation"—the practice of using Western models (like OpenAI or Anthropic) to train Chinese versions, which the U.S. views as potential IP theft.
  • Explosive Startup Growth: Sameer Dholakia (Bessemer Venture Partners) highlighted "unprecedented" growth trajectories in the AI sector.
      • Case Study: Fireworks AI reportedly scaled from $100 million to $1 billion in Annual Recurring Revenue (ARR) in less than 18 months, a pace Dholakia describes as far exceeding the traditional SaaS IPO timeline.
  • Legal Precedents: Anthropic reached a landmark $1.5 billion settlement—the largest in U.S. history for a copyright class action—regarding the use of pirated books to train AI models.

Media Consolidation Hits a Wall

  • Paramount-WBD Merger Paused: A federal judge issued a temporary restraining order just 24 hours before the deal was set to close.
      • Antitrust Risks: Lucas Shaw (Bloomberg) reports that regulators are concerned about a combined entity controlling 25-35% of the film business and an outsized share of the cable network market.
      • Financial Penalties: Paramount faces "ticking fees" amounting to millions of dollars per day if the deal is not finalized by the end of September.
  • Political Context: Jessica Melugin (Competitive Enterprise Institute) characterized the legal challenge as a "political football," noting that 12 Democratic Attorneys General are leading the charge against the merger.

Geopolitical Tensions and National Security

  • Trump-Lebanon Bilateral: President Trump met with the Lebanese President for the first time in nearly 20 years to discuss the Israeli troop withdrawal from Southern Lebanon and the strengthening of the Lebanese Army.
  • Hardline Iran Stance: Trump stated the U.S. has "no interest" in meeting with Iranian leadership until they are ready to talk in a "meaningful way."
      • Maritime Security: Trump promised to "take care of" the Houthi-backed blockade threats against Saudi Arabian ports in the Red Sea.
  • Domestic Safety Claims: Trump claimed Washington D.C. has seen a 92% reduction in crime over the last 15 months, attributing the shift to federal enforcement and the removal of "radical lunatics."

Corporate Strategy and ROI

  • Tesla’s AI Spending Gap: Investors are expressing concern that Tesla has only spent $2.5 billion of its projected $25 billion 2026 CapEx budget.
      • The "Show Me" Quarter: Jordan Fitzgerald (Bloomberg) notes that if Tesla wants to be valued as an AI company rather than a car company, it must prove it is spending at Big Tech levels on robotaxis and robotics.
  • Hyperscaler Valuation: Stephanie Aliaga (JPMorgan) argues that despite high CapEx, hyperscale companies (Alphabet, Meta, etc.) are trading at their lowest valuations since early 2023, suggesting the market may be near an inflection point for rewarding AI monetization.

***

Notable Quotes

"They were keen to slay the dragon, this lingering myth that there is somehow a problem with the [Vera Rubin] rollout. They said, 'we're going to go faster, everything's working.'" — Ian King, Bloomberg News

Is Walmart Backing America’s Next Financial Super-App? | Compound Interest

Semafor(29.4K)·36:32·173 views·2026-07-21
Omer IsmailCEO, OnePay Liz HoffmanHost, Compound Interest Rohan NadkarniHost, Compound Interest
TL;DROne (formerly OnePay) is a Walmart-backed "financial super-app" leveraging a massive, built-in distribution network of 150 million monthly shoppers and 1.5 million employees to provide integrated banking, credit, and investment services with virtually zero customer acquisition costs.

TL;DR

One (formerly OnePay) is a Walmart-backed "financial super-app" leveraging a massive, built-in distribution network of 150 million monthly shoppers and 1.5 million employees to provide integrated banking, credit, and investment services with virtually zero customer acquisition costs.

Scale and the "Super-App" Strategy

  • Growth Metrics: In four years, One has reached 7 million monthly active customers and processes approximately $50 billion in annualized payment flow.
  • The "One-Stop Shop": CEO Omer Ismail describes the platform as an "open-loop" financial ecosystem designed to help users manage money holistically, moving beyond the single-product focus of early fintechs.
  • Product Suite: The app currently offers digital banking, credit building, high-yield savings, installment loans, credit cards, and investment tools (including crypto).
  • Usage Depth: One focuses on "high-engagement" products—the "Google toothbrush test" of items used daily. Currently, users average more than two features per person, with a goal of reaching five or six.

The Distribution Moat: Walmart’s Ecosystem

  • Zero CAC (Customer Acquisition Cost): While most fintechs struggle with high marketing costs, One acquires customers for free through Walmart’s existing digital and physical infrastructure.
  • Associate Integration: Walmart employees (associates) can sign up for One in "20 seconds" during HR onboarding. Walmart shares data (with permission) to pre-fill applications, creating the fastest onboarding in the industry.
  • Point-of-Sale Acquisition: Shoppers on Walmart.com are prompted to use One for installment loans at checkout, converting retail traffic directly into banking customers.
  • Open-Loop Ambitions: While Walmart is the primary anchor, One is expanding into other employer ecosystems (e.g., UKG and Workday) to reach an additional 30–40 million employees.

Operational Structure and Partnerships

  • Independent Governance: Though Walmart is the majority owner, One operates as an independent company outside the "four walls" of the retail giant. This structure was designed by Walmart CEO Doug McMillon and the board (including OpenAI CFO Sarah Friar) to ensure agility and talent retention.
  • Asset-Light Model: One is not a bank. It utilizes a partner-banking model to avoid the heavy regulatory and capital requirements of a banking license:
      • Banking Partners: Coastal Community Bank and Lead Bank.
      • Credit & Loans: Partnered with Klarna (installment loans) and Synchrony (credit cards).
      • Brokerage: Partnered with DriveWealth for clearing and execution.
  • Data Integration: The platform uses "North Star" principles to never ask a customer for information the ecosystem already possesses, using Walmart’s data to personalize financial offers.

Competitive Positioning and Market Outlook

  • Lessons from Goldman Sachs’ "Marcus": Ismail, who previously led Marcus, noted that One is different because it focuses on high-frequency "front door" products (banking/credit) rather than niche, "set-it-and-forget-it" products like high-yield savings or personal loans.
  • The "Rabbit" Culture: Ismail describes the company culture via a fable where a wolf hunts a rabbit: "The wolf was running for his dinner; the rabbit was running for its life." This emphasizes the urgency of a startup despite having a Fortune 1 backer.
  • Market Share: Despite the rise of fintech, legacy banks still hold the vast majority of the market. Ismail argues that One’s distribution edge and multi-product integration allow it to compete where other "point-solution" fintechs have hit a ceiling.

Notable Quotes

"We have the ability to acquire customers at zero CAC [Customer Acquisition Cost] through the Walmart ecosystem... it’s not just distribution that’s a competitive moat, it's also the ability to use data with the customer's permission to offer them products catered to them." — Omer Ismail, CEO of One
"I am a big believer that distribution beats content. You don’t have to have a perfect product—you have to have a good product, but you have to get it to people in a way that makes sense for them and that is cheap for you." — Liz Hoffman, Semafor Business & Finance Editor
"One big lesson is structure... [One] is not inside of [Walmart's] four walls... which has allowed us to be able to get the business to the stage where it's at, with the right team and the right incentives in place." — Omer Ismail, CEO of One

Really the idea was that if you think about Walmart, 200 million shoppers, two and a half million people who work at Walmart, 90% of the U.S. lives within 10 miles of a Walmart. This incredibly large ecosystem and the idea of creating an independent company that is outside the four walls of Walmart, backed by Walmart and backed by Ribbit Capital, to be able to acquire customers into an open-loop financial services platform that can help customers manage their money holistically. You can get access to credit in the form of a credit card or an installment loan. And today, roughly that number—again, zero four years ago because we hadn't started—is roughly $50 billion on an annualized basis. That's the amount of debit spend or credit spend or other forms of payment flow that our customers are doing through our platform. How much of what you're offering in the app did you build yourselves? Is it native to you, versus your wrapper for something else that you buy off the shelf and plug in in the back? One product principle that we have, just to bring this to life, is we never want to ask the customer the answer to a question we already have. So for example, if you're a One Pay digital banking customer and you now want to sign up for the credit card, we already have all the information on you. We know your name, we know your address, we know your social security number. Maybe we need to ask you your income as an example because we don't have that or something else, but we'll never ask you the answers to a question we already have. And so those are the types of north stars that we never wanted to compromise on. The second is just—I know this sounds like incredibly easy to do—but how do you create products that are actually simple and personalized and give customers value? Again, something we did not want to compromise on. When you then go down to how do you want to offer those products, it's a very case-by-case decision as to how much of the stack do we own versus do we want to partner with someone in terms of offering it. If you think about the extremes, digital banking as an example: we are not a bank ourselves. We partner with licensed banks, Coastal Community Bank and Lead Bank. But if you think about the stack, we own a lot of it, again, other than being the actual licensed bank. So in terms of fraud, operations, onboarding, servicing, those are things that our team does. If you think about our invest product as an example, we decided to become an introducing broker-dealer again because we did not want to compromise on those north stars. But we've partnered with DriveWealth, which is a clearing broker-dealer where they own a lot of the trading and the execution and things that we don't want to own. Credit is another example that's probably a little bit somewhere in the middle. Our experience, but we decided strategically that we did not want to own the risk and the balance sheet. And so our brand, our experience, we own the customer data in a way that we can offer this integrated experience. But we've partnered with Klarna. I think there are a couple of different competitive moats, Liz, that we are very focused on that we believe give us a right to win. The first is access to distribution. We have the ability to acquire customers at zero CAC through the Walmart ecosystem. And by the way, it's not just distribution that's a competitive moat. It's where they go sideways. It's just too expensive to get customers, right? It's way too expensive. The second thing I'd say about large banks and where we think about fintech competing with large banks: there's no question that the first chapter of fintech has been about lots of point-to-point solutions, single-product companies that have done quite nicely—digital banking, buy now, pay later, ability to invest, ability to buy crypto. But as customer acquisition costs have continued to go high, and by the way, there's a lot of research that shows customers are overwhelmed by having to manage 15, 20, 25 different financial services relationships. So, the idea of being able to consolidate them in a one-stop shop, there's overwhelming research that shows customers want that. And then the final thing I'd say about fintech and just where we are in the journey, despite everything that's happened over the last 10-15 years and customers being pretty unhappy and low NPS scores with banks and legacy financial services institutions, fintech is still only 3%, 4%, 5% of the digital banking market share. And the highest number in fintech market share is payments, which is at 10%. So I believe, we believe, we're kind of still a little bit in the first innings of where this goes over the course of the next few years and decades. Isn't there a grimmer view of this that there may be some behavior here that's just too sticky to change? I don't believe it's going to be a winner-take-all market. So, I do believe that legacy financial services institutions and banks will continue to exist and grow for sure. But I do believe that part of the issue has been just how hard it is to acquire customers. Why make this an independent thing instead of doing this in-house? I will let Walmart answer that question. I don't want to speak for Walmart. I can speak to One Pay's strategy. You know, Walmart is a majority owner of this company by virtue of the fact that we acquire customers at Walmart. And for our open-loop products like our credit card and our debit card, we offer extra value proposition for our customers to use our products there. You get extra rewards. Our customers are loyal to Walmart, but they spend everywhere. And that has been the strategy from the start. How do you make money? We have recurring income that is transaction-driven. For our banking customers, we have deposits. We share some of that obviously with our bank partners, but we make money on deposits. Our products are fee-free, but for certain features there might be a fee associated with it. So it tends to be a combination of recurring transactional revenue, money on things like deposits, and then some fee revenue. ALL-IN Podcast Live: Mati Staniszewski, Co-Founder & CEO of ElevenLabs | RAISE Summit 2026

RAISE Summit(1.7K)·32:22·2 views·2026-07-21
Jason CalacanisHost, All-In Podcast Mati StaniszewskiCo-Founder & CEO, ElevenLabs
TL;DRElevenLabs has achieved an extraordinary $600M ARR within 40 months of launch, positioning itself as the leader in voice AI by maintaining an engineer-heavy, "No-PM" organizational structure and focusing on high-fidelity, interactive enterprise voice agents.
Revenue...

TL;DR

ElevenLabs has achieved an extraordinary $600M ARR within 40 months of launch, positioning itself as the leader in voice AI by maintaining an engineer-heavy, "No-PM" organizational structure and focusing on high-fidelity, interactive enterprise voice agents.

Rapid Financial Scaling and Growth Metrics

  • Revenue Velocity: CEO Mati Staniszewski reported a massive acceleration in Annual Recurring Revenue (ARR) milestones:
      • $0 to $100M: ~20 months.
      • $100M to $200M: ~10 months.
      • $200M to $300M: ~5 months.
      • Current Status: $600M ARR.
  • Headcount and Attrition: The company currently employs 600 people. Notably, there has been zero attrition among the original 10-person core research and engineering team.
  • Enterprise Adoption: The most recent surge in growth is driven by enterprise sales, particularly in telecommunications, financial services (e.g., Klarna, Revolut), and healthcare.

The "No-PM" Organizational Strategy

  • The Zero-PM Model: ElevenLabs does not employ Product Managers (PMs). Instead, they optimize for "multi-disciplinary experts"—individuals who are experts in one field (coding, design, or customer relations) and advanced in at least one other.
  • Embedded Engineering: Every non-technical department—including Legal and Talent—has dedicated engineers embedded within the team.
      • Primary Roles: These engineers build internal automations and act as a "security check" for AI-deployed software to prevent leaks or technical debt.
  • Small Team Structure: The organization operates via 5-to-10 person "pods" that function autonomously to increase speed and maintain culture during hyper-growth.

Product Innovation and Voice Interactivity

  • The Shift to Voice Agents: ElevenLabs is moving beyond simple text-to-speech into "orchestration," building voice agents that handle real-time interactions, training, and sales.
  • Consumer Behavior: Staniszewski notes a shift in how humans interact with AI:
      • Users feel less "social friction" or shame when discussing sensitive topics (like debt) with AI.
      • Users are more "snappy" and direct, frequently interrupting AI agents to get to the point faster, which requires sophisticated "turn-taking" technology.
  • Accessibility and Social Impact: The company has prioritized "voice restoration" for individuals who lost their speech due to ALS or cancer, highlighting a partnership with U.S. Congresswoman Jennifer Wexton.

Competitive Positioning and Intellectual Property

  • Model Agnosticism: While competing with OpenAI and Anthropic, ElevenLabs allows customers to choose their preferred LLM (OpenAI, Claude, or Open Source) while using ElevenLabs specifically for the voice interaction layer.
  • The "Interaction Layer" Moat: Staniszewski argues that "architecture matters more than scale" in audio. ElevenLabs uses 1,000+ contractors to label audio data to maintain a lead in audio fidelity over general frontier models.
  • Voice Marketplace and IP:
      • Creator Payouts: The company has paid out over $22 million to voice actors and talent who license their voices on the platform.
      • Celebrity Partnerships: High-profile deals include Matthew McConaughey and the estate of James Earl Jones (Darth Vader) for interactive gaming and media use.
  • Safeguards: To combat impersonation, the company uses a three-tier system: tracing all generated audio, moderating text/voice input for scams, and providing public tools to detect ElevenLabs-generated AI audio.
"We don’t have any PMs. We never did. We optimize for profiles that are experts in at least one field—coding, customer, or design—but understand at least one other field really well." — Mati Staniszewski
"It took us roughly 20 months to get to the first 100 million in ARR. Roughly 10 months to get to 200, 5 months to get to 300... and now we are at 600." — Mati Staniszewski
"Voice is identity. It’s the first time technology allows a voice to be carried across languages while exactly preserving the emotional elements." — Mati Staniszewski

ALL-IN Podcast Live: Mark Cuban | RAISE Summit 2026

RAISE Summit(1.7K)·40:52·4 views·2026-07-21
Jason CalacanisHost, All-In Podcast Mark CubanEntrepreneur and Investor
TL;DRWhile AI is the most impactful technology in history, its implementation is significantly harder than anticipated, requiring a shift from text-based LLMs to video-driven "world models" and a strategic return to public markets to facilitate industry consolidation.
Bubble...

TL;DR

While AI is the most impactful technology in history, its implementation is significantly harder than anticipated, requiring a shift from text-based LLMs to video-driven "world models" and a strategic return to public markets to facilitate industry consolidation.

Market Dynamics and the AI "Bubble"

  • Bubble Comparison: Unlike the 1990s .com bubble, today's leaders (Google, Meta) have massive cash flow and real revenue. However, a "bubble" exists within VC and Private Equity due to over-deployment at peak entry prices.
  • Pricing to Perfection: Large tech firms are borrowing hundreds of billions for CapEx and data centers, assuming massive future utilization.
      • Risk of Overcapacity: If technological breakthroughs reduce power requirements or utilization stalls, today’s data centers could become "the dark fiber of the AI era," essentially becoming obsolete infrastructure.
  • The IPO Strategy: Mark Cuban urges companies to go public at smaller valuations ($50M–$100M) rather than staying private.
      • Stock as Currency: Public stock provides a currency for M&A. As AI disrupts legacy businesses, agile companies will need that currency to acquire domain expertise and data without constantly raising expensive cash.

The Reality of AI Implementation

  • Enterprise Friction: AI is harder to implement than the market expected. While individual productivity has spiked, enterprise-level deployment is "terrifying" for CEOs who lack technical understanding.
  • The Labor Myth: Despite predictions that AI would eliminate 50% of white-collar jobs within two years, employment is growing. The demand for "AI-literate" workers currently outstrips the pace of displacement.
  • Need for Human "Harnesses": The current requirement for "forward-deployed engineers" to make AI work at companies like Microsoft or Palantir proves that AI is not yet autonomous.
      • Agent Brittleness: Current AI agents often "drift" or break because they cannot adapt when the underlying LLM updates.

The Next Wave: World Models and Video

  • Limitations of Text: Cuban argues that the future of AI is not in text or pictures. Current models lack "situational physics" (e.g., a child knows a sippy cup will fall; an AI does not).
  • Video-Based Learning: Training on video and "world models" is the next frontier.
      • Investment Focus: Cuban is investing in companies like Matter.com (satellite spectrography) and Synthesia.io to build models that understand the physical world through video rather than just linguistic patterns.
  • Robotics Integration: This shift to world models is the primary prerequisite for functional, widespread robotics.

Policy, Health, and Entrepreneurial Migration

  • Truth-Seeking vs. Engagement: Cuban believes LLMs may save political discourse. Unlike social media (which optimizes for engagement and polarization), LLMs must optimize for "truth-seeking" to maintain user trust.
  • Self-Directed Healthcare: AI-driven medical tools (e.g., Open Evidence) allow for "benchmarking" personal health through blood tests and wearable data, moving 95% of medicine away from "guessing."
  • The Texas Migration: Entrepreneurs are moving from Silicon Valley to Texas (specifically Austin and Dallas) due to a "pressure release" in housing costs and a more permissive regulatory environment for building.
      • The "Valley Vibe": Calacanis and Cuban agree that Silicon Valley has become distracted by "pomp and circumstance" (Series A/B/C rankings) rather than pure execution.

Notable Quotes

"If you need to have forward-deployed engineers, that tells you all you need to know about AI. By definition, you should just be able to ask AI to do what you need and tell it to implement it. Yet Microsoft is hiring 6,000 people to do just that." — Mark Cuban
"Every single motherfucking business plan ever written in the history of business plans is wrong... It’s a thought exercise. So what if the AI is wrong? You’re going to learn and iterate anyway." — Mark Cuban
"Large language models have to be as literal and honest as they possibly can. Their currency is getting you the correct answer. Social media’s currency is keeping you engaged. Those are two totally different missions." — Mark Cuban
"The difference between those AI-first employees and the non-AI-first employees—the gap is so stark. It’s like when someone knew how to use the Office suite and everyone else was still on legal pads." — Jason Calacanis
