Factory's Matan Grinberg: The Coming ‘Dark Factory’ Where Software Builds Itself

Sequoia Capital(232.0K)·51:31·2.7K views·2026-07-21
Matan GrinbergCo-founder and CEO, Factory
TL;DRFactory CEO Matan Grinberg argues that software development is shifting toward a "Dark Factory" model where 90% of code is generated asynchronously by autonomous agents.
He contends that a model-agnostic harness is technically superior to the proprietary "co-design"...

TL;DR

Factory CEO Matan Grinberg argues that software development is shifting toward a "Dark Factory" model where 90% of code is generated asynchronously by autonomous agents. He contends that a model-agnostic harness is technically superior to the proprietary "co-design" approach of labs like OpenAI, as it prevents overfitting and allows enterprises to avoid expensive vendor lock-in.

The Evolution of the "Dark Factory"

  • The Asynchronous Shift: Grinberg predicts that within 12–24 months, 90% of coding tokens will be asynchronous. Unlike current "co-pilot" modes that require human prompting, these agents will operate independently—fixing bugs and creating first-pass solutions while "the lights are off."
  • Industrializing Software: Most large organizations currently operate "artisanal," inefficient software processes with low visibility. The "Software Factory" vision involves codifying tribal knowledge into mathematical assembly lines where outcomes are measured by data rather than "vibes."
  • The Machine Building the Machine: Inspired by Tesla’s highly automated factories, Factory's "Droids" act as robotic arms for code, moving the discipline from manual sewing and woodworking to an industrial, agent-native era.

Strategic Model Agnosticism vs. Lab Lock-In

  • Overfitting Prevention: Grinberg makes the contrarian case that a multi-model harness is superior to a model-and-harness co-design. Exposing a harness to many models prevents it from becoming overfit to the nuances of a single provider (e.g., the friendliness of Claude vs. the meticulousness of GPT).
  • Avoiding "Cloud Scars": Enterprises are wary of the "data gravity" trap experienced with AWS and Azure, where initial subsidies led to 10x price increases at renewal. Model independence allows CIOs to "hot swap" models as new ones become faster or cheaper.
  • Open-Weight Parity: Factory sees a massive shift toward open-weight models like GLM 5.2.
      • Performance: These models are often "frontier minus one" generation—highly performant for 80% of tasks at a fraction of the cost.
      • The "Einstein" Analogy: Grinberg notes that while Einstein (Frontier models) is great for physics, you don’t need him to tutor a child in basic algebra (routine coding tasks).

Rationalizing the "Token Cannon"

  • From Token Maxing to Rationalization: The industry is moving from "Phase 3" (using expensive models like Opus for everything, including checking the weather) to "Phase 4" (cost rationalization).
  • Dynamic Routing: The Factory Router allows organizations to route tasks based on complexity and cost.
      • Example: "Vibe-coding" a dashboard might use Gemini Flash, while a mission-critical COBOL migration might use a specific fine-tuned model.
  • The Future of Pricing: While currently usage-based, Grinberg anticipates a shift toward outcome-based pricing. This would involve a marketplace where model providers "bid" on tasks based on validation criteria (e.g., passing a specific test suite).

Culture of Resilience: The "Journey in the Desert"

  • The $2 Million Refund: Early in the company's history, Factory reached nearly $2M in revenue through strong enterprise sales, but the product wasn't making developers "obsessed."
      • Strategic Integrity: Grinberg proactively handed the money back to customers, a "leap of faith" intended to preserve long-term trust until the product met their internal standards.
  • Output vs. Input Metrics: Factory rejects Amazon’s "customer obsession" (an input) in favor of "creating obsessed customers" (an output).
      • The Basketball Analogy: A coach shouldn't tell players to "make sure to sweat" (input); they should tell them to "score points" (output). If they score, they will naturally sweat.
  • Early Mover Risk: Factory started building autonomous agents in April 2023. Grinberg admits that being two years early is "indistinguishable from being wrong" because the market and procurement behaviors were not yet ready for full autonomy.

Notable Quotes

"Being two or three years early is the same as being wrong. There's no consolation prize. It's either you do the thing or you don't do the thing." — Matan Grinberg
"Our job is to build something so good that our customers themselves become obsessed with us. Customer obsession is an input metric; we care about the output." — Matan Grinberg
"Every CIO is going to need to answer for every incremental token... they are going to have to ask, 'Do you put that incremental dollar towards headcount or towards tokens?'" — Matan Grinberg
"The more models you expose to a harness, the more you avoid overfitting that harness to the nuances of that model in particular." — Matan Grinberg
Bezos at Amazon, it's customer obsession. But in our mind, that's an input metric. Like you don't want to measure input metrics. It doesn't matter if you're customer obsessed. Like you could be customer obsessed and they file a restraining order against you because they don't like what it is that you're doing. Like our job is to build something so good that our customers themselves become obsessed with us. That is our job. It's like, you know, the analogy is if you're a coach of a basketball team, you don't want to tell your players before they come out there, "Hey guys, make sure to sweat." It's like what? No. Score points. We need to score points. And in doing so, yeah, you're probably going to sweat. And I think similarly, to create obsessed customers, you probably need to be really obsessed yourself with the customers. But the output is what matters. We're here in the studio with Matan from Factory. This is our second time with Matan. Yes, indeed. Thanks for having me. You're in the small and elite group of second-time Training Data attendees, so thank you. Matan is the co-founder and CEO of Factory, which makes Droids, which are autonomous agents for the art of software development. Yes, indeed. And Matan, we're going to jump right in because I think you guys are a little bit of a dark horse candidate in this world of software development. It is a market that has absolutely taken off. There are folks like Claude Code and Cognition and others who have a lead, but you guys are coming up strong. Talk about the competitive dynamics and what makes Factory special. It's been a wild ride. We started Factory three and a half years ago now. So in April of 2023, when the world and the enterprise in particular was barely ready for GitHub Copilot, let alone fully autonomous agents. I think the first two years were kind of our journey in the desert, as I like to refer to it, because we were focused on fully autonomous agents, but engineers weren't ready and procurement teams at the enterprise weren't ready. Retrospectively, we really honed our craft and learned a lot about how to build for developers in the enterprise. But you know, it took a lot of time to actually come around to when they were ready to receive it. And so we're kind of now emerging much more. Some of these other players like Anthropic or OpenAI who have a ton of distribution are going in and bringing their incredible tools like Claude Code or Codex. The thing that enterprises are really caring about, that we have learned through those two years, is they do not want anyone to be their single point of failure. They do not want anyone to control their fate. And so something that really matters is model independence. Everyone learned from cloud where, you know, back in the cloud days, it was like AWS or Azure being like, "Hey, come on in. Sign this three-year contract. It's going to be so cheap. We're going to subsidize it. It'll be great." And then a couple years later when it came time for renewal, they would 10x the contract and... Haha, data gravity. We got you now. Yeah, we got you. What, are you going to do a two-year migration to go to someone else? Like no way. Everyone has scars from that now. Everyone knows, look, Claude Code is fantastic. Codex from OpenAI is fantastic. We cannot put our fate in any one of these model providers' hands. Also, if you just look at the risk profiles of the model labs versus the cloud providers, what's the last piece of drama that came out of one of the cloud providers versus like the model labs? It seems like there's kind of always some sort of chaos of internal fighting or getting in spats with the government or any other entities. And so if you're going to build this very important part of your business, you want to make sure that you're robust to any of these changes. That's something that we've learned over those kind of initial two years: developers really care about things being modular. They want to know that they can customize it to what they want. They want to know that if there's a new model that comes out that's faster or cheaper or more performant, they can kind of hot swap it in. That's one of the biggest reasons why a lot of the largest enterprises are taking the momentum that they had from a Codex or a Claude Code and are carrying that into Factory because they get that performance from these fantastic models, but they do it without the vendor lock-in that the model labs directly provide. And if I'm the enterprise, I'm going to be like, "Wait a minute. Am I now just getting locked into Factory?" What's the answer to that? So that's a really good question because that is something that you might think of. Like, "Okay, wait. So we're just switching the lock-in point." All of the modularity that we build is such that if at some point you want to say, "Hey, you know what? Factory's not staying at the frontier anymore," whether it's the automations that you build or the skills registry that we help you create, the work that we've done stays in your codebase. Any of the automations that we've created, the artifacts also live in your codebase. In other words, there aren't really things that we're saying like our tribal knowledge about your org that we're keeping on our side and not giving to you. That's part of the relationship that we have with customers; we similarly want to make sure we're providing the best experience possible. If we help you arbitrage between different models to get cost optimization, we're giving you that optimization. We're not taking that away from you. That's a really important part of the trust that we're building with these enterprises. You and I were talking probably a couple months ago at this point, and I was trying to give you credit for having the right vision for this market two, three years ago. And you responded with something along the lines of "Thank you, but being two or three years early is the same as being wrong." Yes. Which I thought was a wonderful response in so many ways. Can you talk about those two years in the desert? How did it feel to have this vision that turned out to be right that nobody appreciated for a year or two? Can you talk about that journey and what it has done to the DNA of your company? Yeah, I mean in the moment, it's really, really difficult because I hadn't had a job before. I dropped out of my PhD to start this company. Over the course of those two years, I convinced 20 of the smartest people that I've ever met to quit what they were doing and join Factory and join us on this mission. These are people with families, these are people with kids who are dedicating years of their lives to this problem. Going customer after customer, they weren't ready for agents, they didn't get it. Also the models weren't as performant, but I think a lot of it was behavioral. And I mean even just a fun anecdote of giving developers an NPS survey. If you ever are giving a developer an NPS survey, they do not like whatever it is that you are giving it to them because developers, they vote with their feet. They are very clear what they like and what they don't like. If you're like, "Hmm, I wonder if they like it," they definitely don't. During that time, I think there was a lot that we were learning; I myself had never had a job before. Enterprise sales is not something that comes obvious to a physicist. But at the end of the day, it doesn't matter. You don't get any bonus points for being early because who cares? There's no consolation prize. It's either you do the thing or you don't do the thing, and that's all that matters. For the team it was really tough. There were points where we ended up getting good at enterprise sales, but the product still wasn't good. That's a very tricky position to be in because we ended up getting to a point where we were just under two million in revenue and the product was not good. There was a point in time where we realized this. If you're really good at sales, you can sign contracts; you can definitely do that. But if you're doing that and the developers don't like your product, it's a ticking time bomb because eventually they're going to churn and it's going to be really, really bad. We realized this and we proactively gave all of those customers their money back. That was one of the most difficult decisions to make. Not only is there a group of 20 people who are getting ridiculous offers from all the labs, they have these huge financial incentives to go elsewhere, there are all these other companies that are doing well, and they decided to do this. Then we're going to say, "Oh yeah, by the way, that little bit of revenue we managed to get, we're actually going to give it back because we don't think the product is making their developers happy." Why did you make that decision? We sold them on a good vision and convinced them that this is the right team to work with and that we were going to deliver this solution for them. But we realized that the way that we had sold them on it and the product that we were delivering was not up to snuff in a way that I don't think it would hold true to one of our operating principles. And one of our operating principles that I really like is create obsessed customers. This kind of flips over Bezos's thing where Bezos at Amazon, it's customer obsession. But in our mind, that's an input metric. Input metrics are like you don't want to measure input metrics. It doesn't matter if you're customer obsessed. You could be customer obsessed and they file a restraining order against you because they don't like what it is that you're doing. Like our job is to build something so good that our customers themselves become obsessed with us. That is our job. It's like the analogy is if you're a coach of a basketball team, you don't want to tell your players before they come out there, "Hey guys, make sure to sweat." Like no. Score points. We need to score points. And in doing so, yeah, you're probably going to sweat. I think similarly, to create obsessed customers, you probably need to be really obsessed yourself with the customers. But the output is what matters. Coming back to this, the product that we were delivering was not creating obsessed customers. And we wanted to make sure—this was a group of the smartest people I've ever met—we were getting there. We were getting a lot of intuition, things were starting to come together internally. We could see internally we were starting to become a lot more agent-native in how we were doing things and the product was scratching that itch, but we were ahead of our customers. We wanted to maintain trust with our customers so that when it does hit, we can come back to them and say, "Hey guys, this is the real deal, I promise." To build that credibility we had to say, "Hey, look, even though you were maybe happy to continue, we're going to give you this back and say three months from now, I think it'll be ready. Give us some time and I promise we will knock your socks off." How did your customers react when you had that conversation? Some of them were like, "Oh great. Sounds good." Because I think it wasn't something that they were obsessed with. Some of them were a little bit confused, but I think generally, especially with enterprises, they're not used to these things. A lot of times enterprise budget, once it's gone it's gone and no one really cares. Some of them didn't even know if they had a mechanism by which to take back the money. It's a difficult thing to tell also like investors who believe in you. I remember having the conversation with Shaun. I think Shaun obviously stayed really close with the company, so he was very on the same page. But it's kind of a scary thing to be like, "Hey, by the way, remember all those updates and you're saying, 'Hey, look, the revenue is going up'? It's about to go down to zero." It was a scary thing. I think it was kind of a leap of faith of like we see the signal internally early of like this is the direction we need to go, we need to pivot the approach on the product. I remember that all-hands where we told the whole team; it was like one of the worst months of my life. Not everyone was going to say, "What the hell is this? What's going on?" But it's the looks on their faces where they kind of go a little bit pale and they're like, "Oh boy, is this just the early signs and we're about to sink completely?" How'd you keep the team together through that? Honestly, the only reason the team stayed together is we were so ruthless about hiring early on. It was like people that are genuinely really, really obsessed with the mission, which our mission is to bring autonomy to software engineering. Really caring about that, making sure everyone was also very clear on feedback loops as to like the fate is in our hands. It's not like this is something that I go do. It's like we all have a part to play in making this work. Embracing how much it sucked was also something that was very valuable. Just being honest about it. Being super honest about, "Yeah, this sucks. Look at those competitors, their revenue is going up like crazy. This is not good. We are in a very bad position. We just had to give back all of our revenue. We need to really get our shit together." In the moment, I think retrospectively those are the moments where really the deepest bonds are made. If you talk to people who are athletes or even academics, whenever you're in the stressful period, whether it's cramming before finals or in intense—we have some rowers on our team and I think that's an example we always go to—that sport sucks. It's pure pain. There is one number that quantifies your performance, just what is your time on your 2K. Embracing that is what creates those enduring bonds such that afterwards we know what it's like to be at rock bottom. We know what it's like to lose. When we first started the company our valuation was 5 million. A lot of our competitors, a lot of the companies out there these days, they don't know what it's like to not be a unicorn. That is what they are day one. Whereas we have been there in those dark moments and not a single person left. That makes us so resilient and so strong that going forward, things are going a lot better now. There are going to be really bad times, but we have that resiliency in our DNA that I'm not sure some of these other companies do. I love that. Talk to us about what changed. I'm curious about your comment from earlier that the models getting better is not the most important thing that happened. Because at least in my mind, the models getting better is the most important thing that happened. Help me understand. Yeah, so a couple things. One is the interaction pattern that we were building for before was too ambitious. To your point, we were right in that what we were building for was fully autonomous agents, but it was two years too early, which makes it wrong. Fully autonomous agents require a complete change in behavior from the developer. We were trying to do that out of the box before they were even using tools like Copilot. It was just too much of a leap, too much of a step-function jump. September 26, 2025, was when we first put out the Droids CLI. The Droids CLI met developers where they were in a manner that previously these fully autonomous agents did not. Its performance was state of the art and it was model agnostic, so it could use every model that was out there. September 26 was also two years after we initially started, so the world had gotten much more used to using things like autocomplete. By late 2025, most engineers were using an autocomplete tool and many were starting to use a chat interface to ask an agent to go do changes wholesale. However, what we see is that if you go back now and use these agentic interactions with older models, they're still good. The biggest thing that changed was developers and in particular in the enterprise, being open-minded to this new way of working. Developers have established their workflows over the last 30 years; they can be stubborn. A lot of them were like, "No, my craft could never be done by an AI tool." A lot of it was just understanding how to work with these tools and having the willingness to go in and try, and also the intuition about what are the guardrails that you need to provide in order for it to succeed. I think it was a combination of both of these things. The models getting better so you need to do less in the way of providing guardrails. But also developers lowering their guard and being like, "Okay, you know what? Let me try and do these things." There's a certain degree to which when Andrej Karpathy tweets about something, then every engineer suddenly is like, "Okay, maybe this is true." And Andrej started to tweet about these agentic workflows. Early on he wasn't as open to it. Him being more open to it genuinely changed some people's minds. It's funny, but that's some of the things that go into behavior changes. You hear it from people you trust, you start seeing it from people within your organization who are maybe a little bit more agent-native. That is what changed. And now we're all going to be on Slack. We might be pushing the limits of Slack, which I think is going to be another interesting thing. Okay, September 2025 you launched the Droids CLI. You said frontier performance SOTA. What does that mean for you? There are the benchmarks, which have a very short half-life. Any time there's a good benchmark, it gets benchmark-maxed within three to six months. At the time, the one that we championed when we launched, which ended up becoming a pretty good benchmark, was TerminalBench. Prior to that, the one leading was SWE-bench, which took some open-source projects and examples of issues that were then solved. The problem with that was it was very focused on Python and scripting or individual file changes. Whereas TerminalBench was in the terminal setting, so it was things like scheduling runs and tasks that were not just changing the code file but general software development. That was something that we ended up having really frontier performance on. Now it's benchmark-maxed to the extreme where I think models that come out now are 90% on it. There's a very short time horizon from putting out a good benchmark to then it being in the training data. What goes into building a great harness? It seems like there's almost a lot of FUD in the ecosystem of "my harness is better than your harness." You need to own the model to have a good harness or actually you have a better harness if you don't own the model. What's your mental model for what keeps you at the frontier? Some general things that matter are the way you do caching. Cache tokens end up being a tenth as expensive, so one big piece of performance for a given harness is what is your rate of token caching? Another example is how do you perform while in compression or compaction? When you're dealing with a long session you're going to exceed the context limit of the model itself. The harness will do some sort of summarization, compression, or compaction. The way that you perform during that compaction is a big determining factor of how good your harness is. Tests that they do for that are like "needle in the haystack," where you have some long thread and maybe there's one piece of information that's really important. How often will your harness preserve that through compaction? Other examples are tool use or how it uses the environment to validate whatever work that it's doing. These are things that you can have individual metrics on and we have our own internal benchmarks to measure how do the out-of-the-box agents do versus how does Factory perform. Naively, everyone believed initially that if you train the model and you build the harness you're going to make them better together. Much to the chagrin of many of my friends at OpenAI and Anthropic, this is not true. If you build a harness that supports different models, that harness will be better. My intuition would be model-harness co-design makes you better. What's the intuition for why it's actually not? It's analogous to the idea maybe 10 years ago: if you were to be like, "I want to train my personal AI," back in early ML days before GPT-3, "I'm going to give it all of my data because I want it to know me." Turns out the answer was train it on the whole internet and it will be so much better for you than if it were just trained on your data. There is a sort of analog that emerges where it's what data is to a model, models are to a harness. Where the more models you expose to a harness, you avoid overfitting that harness to the nuances of that model in particular. There are certain intricacies about different models that you can learn from and then improve different models' performance in your own harness. This was why we stopped doing it—because TerminalBench got benchmark-maxed—but initially when every new Opus or GPT model would come out, it would perform better on TerminalBench in Droids than it would in Claude Code or Codex. Ideally, from a lab perspective, you want it so that it's better together because then that means you have to use their harness and you can't use a different one. But the reality is having that multi-model harness ends up getting frontier performance on all of those. Is there a good example or illustration of that? Conceptually it makes sense. Is there an easy way to illustrate it? Maybe a good example is if you're familiar with the different behaviors of Opus and GPT-5.6 right now. Opus tends to be, loosely, that super friendly colleague where you're like, "I want to do these 20 tasks," and they're like, "Okay cool. Hey, by the way, five of those tasks I realized we didn't need to do, don't worry about it. I got the others done, did it this way, it was great." It'll be like, "Tonight's a good time. Let's pick it up in the morning." Yeah, "Let's go get a beer afterwards." Meanwhile, GPT-5.6 is like, "Absolutely, I will do every single one of those and nothing will stop me." It's very OCD and meticulous. Sometimes you want one where it realizes that list of 20 you gave me, there's a better way of doing it anyway. If you build a harness for each of those, there are different things that that harness will be good or bad at. For example, agents will often generate a to-do list. The Claude Code harness was really strict to make sure it would stick to the to-do list because the model itself would typically wander. Meanwhile, Codex wouldn't do that because the model was really OCD about that. If you're a user, you want to have the same experience regardless. You want to make sure if you switch to a different model you're not going to suddenly lose track of whatever things that you were working on. There were cases where it would just not have the to-do list. The to-do list matters because if you're doing some crazy migration and you don't have it, then you're in this long session where there's compaction, that might get lost, and then now you forgot what your seventh step was. That could be one of the failure modes. It's a great example. Okay, we talked about one type of maxing: benchmark maxing. We want to talk about token maxing. The world has gone from token maxing to cost rationalization. What does that mean for Factory? Loosely, there was phase zero: no one believed in AI. Phase one: everyone believes in AI and boards were like, "Mr. CEO, what's your AI strategy?" The CEO is like, "I don't know, CTO, make sure everyone uses AI." Phase two: the CTO says, "Let's start putting it in performance reviews and make public rankings of who's using tokens the most because everyone's skeptical." Then we enter phase three: everyone sees these ratings, they see it's part of their perf reviews, and they're like, "Okay, I'm going to use AI for everything." That's token maxing, where people are using Opus for literally everything. Banks are working with us where they are spending hundreds of thousands of dollars a month on people asking, "What's the weather in SF?" or "Tell me about Python." Trivial questions you could Google, people are asking Opus. We overcorrected and were like, "adoption by any means necessary." I think that's a decent approach—it's faster to do that and then curb usage—but where Factory fits in is the Factory Router. It allows you to dynamically route to different models based on the task. If you're asking what the weather is, you probably don't need the very frontier of human intelligence. Also, for every enterprise, 12 months from now, not everyone needs the same tokens. Having a blanket token cap for every individual makes no sense. Every CIO is going to need to answer for every incremental token. Right now, we're saying PMs who are "vibe-coding" dashboards get the same token limits as engineers building critical infrastructure. That's probably not the best thing. Or you might be dealing with COBOL codebases where Opus is not the best model to use, but maybe some fine-tuned model on that codebase in particular. The point of the router is that we can accommodate these different constraints. Maybe this part of the org can use Gemini Flash, this part does COBOL, so we route to that model. Maybe this other part, we really care about reliability, so let's generate the code with OpenAI, test it with Anthropic, review it with Gemini. We take in your routing procedure instructions in natural language. We'll do internal benchmarking to know which models are better at which of these tasks. How close are the open models at this point? Which one's the best? GLM 5.2 is incredible. It's at the point where internally we have no token limits for our engineers, and half of our tokens are open models. They're faster, cheaper, just as performant. Everyone is comparing GLM 5.2 to the latest model like Opus 4.8 or GPT 5.6. But really they should be compared to Opus 4.7 or GPT 5.5, because the open models are generally a generation behind. Is the open model getting as good as frontier minus one? Unequivocally yes. It's great for consumers of APIs. If you're a business doing software engineering, your job is to solve problems. If we can allow you to solve those problems faster and with cheaper models, that's a good thing. It's a world where there is not a monopoly on intelligence but a garden of intelligence that you can pick and choose from. If you're trying to get a tutor for your daughter in algebra, you can probably find someone cheaper than Albert Einstein to be that tutor. If you look at the pie chart of the complexity of models being used by your customer base, what did it look like a few months ago? What does it look like today? Enterprises haven't gone too opinionated into the routing procedures yet. This is something that will happen over the next 6 to 12 months. At the beginning of the year, it was less than 1% of tokens. In the first quarter, it became a single-digit percent. It has now crossed into being a double-digit percent of tokens. Percent of tokens is not always the same as percent of cost because open tokens are cheaper, but it's pretty crazy to see the growth. What's your forecast? My sense is that we will asymptote towards a vast majority being open. That's of token share, not necessarily of leverage share. Maybe there's 1% of tokens that are incredibly valuable and key for decision-making, while the rest are implementation tokens. I don't think it'll ever be 100%. The frontier of intelligence will inherently always be valuable for every business. But we'll be better at offloading certain tasks. It's analogous to how human engineers work. How much brain power are they using? A lot of it is low-leverage. All of those low-leverage moments we want to automate away. If you're an engineer writing docs, that is such a low-leverage use of your time. Imagine all the other stuff those incredible engineers could do if it wasn't writing documentation. We should live in a world where everyone can have docs as good as Stripe. That is strictly beneficial. Given the rise of open-weight models, how do you think about business model and pricing? This is more what our customers want and need. Usage-based is clearly the way to go; we want to be aligned with what they are doing. Seat-based doesn't make sense. My sense is that eventually we will change to outcome-based. The enterprise isn't ready for that yet, and we've learned our lesson—we're not going to impose things. But my suspicion is that in the 2030s things will probably look more like outcome-based. What would be the definition of an outcome? Right now we are usage-based; the more tokens you use, the more you pay, the more we get. Since we are model-independent, we are pointing a token cannon at OpenAI, Anthropic, AWS, or GCP. To a certain degree, this is like a dumbed-down version of a marketplace. There's a buyer, the engineer who wants a task done. You have model providers saying, "We perform at this cost and this performance." We determine who we go to. There's a world in which, if it's so important to get these tokens, they might bid in a certain way. They win the bid if they get the task done under the cost with the validation criteria. The way you determine if the task was successful is by some validation loops. No one is using these tools where it's just "write me code, great, thank you." It's "write me code, and here's how I know it was done well." You named the company Factory. Everybody wants to build a software factory. Where are we today, and how close are we to the ultimate vision? Everyone has a software factory whether they know it or not; it's just a very inefficient one. It feels like pre-industrialization, where people were manually sewing things together or woodworking. It's inefficient. If you go to an organization with more than 10,000 people and ask about the process by which they release a feature, there are hundreds or thousands of people in that process, and most likely they couldn't even draw it for you. That's just the nature of building large software today. But with these systems, so much tribal knowledge can be codified. So much that would require a guru who's been here for 30 years can be automated and refocused on what actually moves the needle. This move towards software factories is a move towards how do we figure out the actual inputs that determine what features to build. We have signals, then what is the process? Mapping out the assembly lines is important because then you get to close the loop and say, "Did this actually deliver outcome for our business?" Two years from now, you're going to have to answer: do you put that incremental dollar towards headcount or towards tokens? And if tokens, where in the org? You need quantitative insight to do that. Otherwise, you're just shooting from the hip. 10 years from now, we're going to look back at this era of software and it's going to feel like businesses in ancient times where they didn't do accounting. It'll be like marketing in the day of Mad Men, where it's all creative and you have no idea what's actually working. It makes no sense. It's just, "Yeah, let's ship that feature. Oh, I think it went well." You don't do backprop in a model by vibes; you run those actual calculations. We need that same rigor in software engineering. In the limit, how much will people spend on tokens versus engineering headcount? It'll depend on the business. Salespeople probably don't need many tokens; engineering teams, the more you want people to own an outcome end-to-end, if you give them more tokens, they can produce a lot more. This is an opportunity for every business to double down on their core competency and procure externally whatever it is that doesn't matter for them. But I think that's been something you had to do, not because it's a core competency of the business. Allowing businesses to focus and double down on the things that they are best at is going to be good for the consumers of their business. I think we're just going to see a lot of ruthless refocusing on what actually matters, which is going to be cool to see. Every company kind of has to go through this process of reinvention. Ten or 20 years ago, people talked about digital transformation, and I don't know if anybody's given it a buzzword now, maybe AI transformation or something of that sort. A couple of years ago, you ran into a bunch of organizations that just weren't ready to deal with autonomous agents. Things have changed, and you've seen your customers start to change. So the question is, when you look at your customers as they kind of go up this maturity curve and sort of reinvent themselves for the future, are there any good tricks or techniques that you've seen them use to repot themselves a bit? Yeah, I mean, I think surprisingly, the companies that have been doing company-wide hackathons really end up doing well. It seems like relatively trivial, but just setting aside a day for everyone in the workforce to just build stuff with AI really sets the tone and sets the pace. Su's just given me a look because she... I tried to force him to build something with coding agents; it didn't go so well. We'll work on it. We'll do it after this. I, you know, we gave it a great effort. But that's it—literally just setting aside the time to do it, and even if it fails miserably, like, it's fine. Also, the organizations that are okay with failing are succeeding. I feel like there are some who are like, "We need to do it exactly right. We need to make the right decision from day one, no error." Like, you're going to make mistakes. Everyone is going to. The organizations who are leaning into it and embracing it to a certain degree are succeeding. One of our largest customers is EY. EY is not necessarily known to be at the absolute frontier of AI, but for them, they were just like, "Look, this matters." There have been other transformations that we were late to; we're not going to be late to this. We're just going to go in, we might mess up—obviously respecting the things that you're not allowed to mess up, put those aside—but let's go and get our engineers to mess around and build this stuff and see where it breaks and understand what they like and what they don't like. I think that really matters a lot in the ones that we're seeing succeed. Also, the ones who are pretty bold in reinventing the processes that they've put in place are doing well. They’re saying, "Hey, there's no sacred cows; let's put this aside and try something out." If it doesn't work, we'll put that sacred cow right back. I think that's been a determining factor there. And when it comes from within—if it comes from the board, it's probably not going to go well. If it comes from within the tech team, or the individual contributors, or the leadership, that's when we see it go better. Tiffany, predictions for the most important changes that are going to happen in your space over the next, call it, 12 months? A lot of AI consumption is going up like crazy, and everyone's super excited because the revenues are going wild. A lot of this is synchronous usage. In other words, if everyone woke up sick tomorrow, a lot of Claude code usage would be zero because it's all just "Hey, Claude code" or "Hey, Codex" or "Hey, Droid," right? I think in 12 to 24 months, 90% of tokens will be asynchronous tokens. These are going to be Droids on their own, autonomously saying, "Hey, here's some signal that I found from a customer, let's go fix it," or "Let's go create a first-pass solution to this." I think that is going to be where the real agent-native stuff begins because, right now, we're still kind of in co-pilot mode. If you go to an agent and say, "Hey, go do this for me," it is more agentic because it's not going to come back and ask you a ton, but it's still like you are kicking it off. If you guys have ever been to Tesla's factories—which was one of the sources of inspiration for the name—there are just robotic arms everywhere going and doing stuff. It's not like there are people there going and attaching the widget to the thing. This idea of a dark factory where the lights are off and things are just happening, that is where software development is going. That's kind of where the name came from. Elon was always talking about the factory as the machine that builds the machine. That's been something that we took to heart. And I guess also that's combined with his whole thing about how you're destined to become the opposite of your name. In our case, factory becomes artisanal, which is a good flip there. What's your most optimistic version of the future both for Factory and for the world at large? I think short-term there's going to be a lot of turbulence because I think a lot of companies have misallocated resources pretty poorly. There's been a lot of bloat. I think the correction that's going to happen there is going to be really painful for a lot of people. That's something that I think every AI CEO should bear much more responsibility for than they currently are, and also figure out ways to address and kind of ameliorate that because this is going to be very painful for a lot of people. I have optimism that we can actually address that faster than we think; we just need to start now. Longer-term, why I think this is a good thing—and why I don't believe at all the BS that people are saying about engineers going away—is that there is a huge number of problems in the world. A large subset of those problems can be solved with software, but a small subset is currently being solved with software. In the short term, okay, there was a given problem that was over-allocated engineering resources, so we need to reallocate those. Reallocate is a cold way of saying some people are going to lose their jobs. But the thing that's going to happen in the longer term is we need engineers. Engineers are some of the best systems thinkers and problem solvers. There are so many problems that can be solved with software that are not being solved. We are going to take those engineers and have them go and solve problems that previously were not being solved. That is such a net good for the world. Also, there are so many problems that we are maybe solving but with really shitty software. This is going to enable people to solve them with incredible software. The vision for Factory is that we are the factory that allows them to build this incredible software. These problems range from trivial things to, you know, government software. DMV or IRS websites—all that stuff is generally a pretty poor experience. We don't need to live like that; we can live in a world where all software is really fantastic. Also, things like pharmaceutical research: so much that goes into solving diseases is not just a biology problem; a lot of it requires the best software engineers in the world. Previously, those problems haven't allocated the right dollars to attract the best engineers, but because of what's happening, I think we will be much more closely allocated to the biggest problems. I think it's our job as an industry to do that reallocation as quickly as possible, so it's not 10 years, but maybe six months or a year. Wonderful. Matan, I think the clarity and consistency of your vision over time has just always been very inspiring, and seeing how much you've grown as a leader and how much Factory's grown as a company, even since the last time we did this Training Data episode, it's truly awe-inspiring. So thank you for joining us again to share what you're up to. I appreciate that a lot. Thank you. Thank you.

U.S. Trade Rep. Jamieson Greer: 'Expect action soon' on sweeping new tariffs

CNBC Television(3.4M)·14:34·12.8K views·2026-07-21
Jamieson GreerU.S. Trade Representative
TL;DRU.S.
Trade Representative Jamieson Greer defended the administration's new 50% tariffs on specific Canadian goods as a necessary response to Canadian trade retaliation and signaled that sweeping new global actions regarding forced labor and AI competition are imminent. The...

TL;DR

U.S. Trade Representative Jamieson Greer defended the administration's new 50% tariffs on specific Canadian goods as a necessary response to Canadian trade retaliation and signaled that sweeping new global actions regarding forced labor and AI competition are imminent. The briefing underscores a fundamental shift away from the "NAFTA-era certainty" of offshoring, prioritizing domestic manufacturing and the reduction of the national trade deficit.

Targeted Tariffs on Canada

  • Reciprocal Basis: The 50% tariffs (effective in 30 days) are a direct response to Canadian retaliation against 2025 U.S. trade policies.
      • Specific Grievances: Greer cited Canada’s ban on U.S. liquor sales, caps on auto shipments from companies reshoring to the U.S., and restricted market access for U.S. dairy.
      • Dairy Dispute: While Canada committed to dairy quotas under USMCA, Greer alleges they restrict sales to co-ops owned by Canadian farmers, while granting better retail/wholesale access to the EU.
  • Negotiation Status: Greer characterized Canadian proposals as "promises to discuss" rather than substantive changes. He noted that while talks continue, trade actions are being used to "incentivize" actual policy shifts.

Future of USMCA and North American Reshoring

  • End of "NAFTA Certainty": Greer explicitly rejected the idea that companies should feel "comfortable" offshoring production to Canada or Mexico for re-export to the U.S.
      • Investment Warning: Companies (like GM) building factories in Canada to export back to the U.S. should not expect a 100% guarantee of market access.
      • Policy Goal: The administration’s primary metric is lowering the trade deficit and increasing "North American content" (U.S., Canadian, and Mexican parts) in goods traded within the bloc.
  • USMCA Renewal: President Trump has opted not to renew the USMCA for 16 years, with Greer stating that the administration has already "overwhelmed" elements of the agreement with national security-based actions on steel, aluminum, and autos.

Global Trade Enforcement: AI and Forced Labor

  • Section 301 Investigation: Greer confirmed that "action soon" is expected regarding a massive investigation into forced labor.
      • Scope: The investigation covers 60 countries and addresses the lack of enforcement in other jurisdictions (like the EU) where forced labor laws are not yet in effect.
  • China and AI Competition: The U.S. is monitoring China’s AI advancements as a form of forced technology transfer and IP theft.
      • Unfair Advantages: Greer noted concerns over China "distilling" U.S. AI models, subsidizing energy for AI production, and pushing standards that provide an unfair advantage to Chinese firms.
  • EU/Airbus Subsidy Dispute: The U.S. is closely reviewing a new loan to Airbus. Greer warned that the U.S. will not accept a system where Boeing must compete against "unfair loans" that bypass market-based systems.

State of U.S.-Canada Relations

  • Historical Context: Greer framed current tensions as part of a long history of trade friction regarding softwood lumber, grains, and pork.
  • Diplomatic Tone: Despite the tariffs, Greer described his relationship with Canadian counterparts as good and noted that Prime Minister Trudeau’s recent responses have been "firm but moderate."
"If you're building in America, that's where you're going to have certainty. You're going to have access to the robust American consumer... If you're planning on building a new factory in Canada and exporting back to the United States, I think you have to understand that the American people, they don't want offshoring." — Jamieson Greer
"We've been asking for a year for the Canadians to remove [retaliatory measures], to modify them, to adjust them. They haven't done that. And so we have a tailored action... which is a natural consequence of the Canadian retaliation." — Jamieson Greer
"We fundamentally disagree on NAFTA... That's part of the whole problem is that we don't like that certainty of saying, you're going to go overseas, build a factory and re-export it here." — Jamieson Greer
President Trump signed three proclamations yesterday to impose 50% tariffs on some Canadian goods. Those would take effect in 30 days. Joining us now is Jamison Greer, U.S. Trade Representative. It is good to see you. Good morning. How are you? Mr. Ambassador? We will call you that. How about Jamison? Jamison. All right, well, you have been on many times. Yeah. Good to see you, Joe. I appreciate you having me. A couple of questions come to mind. What was the tipping point to do this in terms of its reciprocal nature? That is something that caught the President's ire. Can you describe exactly what it was and why these very targeted tariffs are affecting hockey sticks and some things that maybe we would not think of immediately? Well, let me give you just a little bit of history, Joe, on this issue. In April of 2025, as you know, the President embarked on a new trade policy really designed at reshoring manufacturing production to the United States and getting the trade deficit down after it exploded for decades with all the countries. We imposed tariffs on the world at varying levels. Of all the countries who retaliated against the United States, one was the People's Republic of China and the other was Canada. Some of the things they did is they banned the sale of U.S. liquor and other booze on Canadian shelves. They capped and limited the amount of autos that could be shipped to the United States from companies who were reshoring to the U.S., and they also continue to maintain limited space for market access for U.S. dairy. But importantly, they give other countries better deals on each of these three issues. And so it was a natural consequence, after a year of these measures being in place—and longer in the case of dairy—for the President to take action. These are responses to this retaliation. We have been asking for a year for the Canadians to remove these, to modify them, or to adjust them. They have not done that. And so we have a tailored action that covers a subset of Canadian goods. We are not banning the goods like the Canadians have banned American alcohol. We are not putting a cap on them or limiting them like they have done to cars and dairy, but we have a tariff, which is a natural consequence of the Canadian retaliation that has endured for a long time. Are either the actions that Canada has been taking or these reciprocal tariffs here allowable under the current reading of the USMCA? So for the dairy, for example, the United States is very competitive in dairy production and cheese, and the Canadians made a commitment to us under the USMCA to allow us access under certain types of quotas. However, in implementing that access, they have said you can only sell essentially to co-ops, which are owned by Canadian dairy farmers who obviously have less of an incentive to get American dairy. But when you look at the EU, they allow EU dairy not only to go to those destinations, but they can also sell them to retail, to wholesale, and to food service. That is the kind of access we expected out of USMCA and that we are not getting. The prohibition on wine and spirits, that is not permitted under our trade agreements. People will come and they will say, "Well, the United States has taken trade actions on Canada," etc. We have taken actions consistent with our national security to make sure that we can reshore and support our auto supply chains, which are under attack by China and excess capacity worldwide. We are protecting our steel and aluminum; other countries are taking similar actions to protect their steel products. So the types of things we are doing are overdue. No one has retaliated against us except China and Canada, so we are taking a targeted measure to counter that retaliation. Mark Carney and the Prime Minister said that the new tariffs are the latest in a series of unilateral U.S. trade actions that do violate the USMCA. Canada has been making a lot of detailed proposals to try to resolve some of these issues and they stand ready to engage. Do you think that is not true? Is he distorting it? The Canadians have always been willing to engage if engagement means talking and meeting. We have done that. I have done that. I have a good relationship with my counterparts there. When he talks about these proposals, I will not go into detail because I want to respect the confidentiality, but what I will say is the proposals are generally promises to discuss these issues—which we are already doing—not to actually change these things. They talk about collaboration on energy. We have never had tariffs on Canadian energy; we have always been open to that and that kind of exploration. So the types of things that have been in these proposals have always been, "Let us talk, let us keep trading in energy," things we already do, things that do not fundamentally commit to resolve these issues. They maybe just talk about them some more, and for us, we want them fixed. What do you think the President feels we should do with USMCA? What will be the end result? Do you know? Well, what I would say, first of all, as you know, the President-elect did not renew USMCA, and in some ways, he has already overwhelmed elements of that agreement with actions he has taken, again, on auto, steel, and aluminum, which are global in nature but obviously affect our relationship with Canada and Mexico. One of the things he wants to see with Canada and Mexico is he wants our deficit to go down with these countries. With Canada, it has gone down over the past year; with Mexico, it has gone up. Our global trade deficit overall, if you are measuring the 12 months before the tariff program started in April 2025 and the 12 months afterward, the trade deficit globally has gone down, which is good, but the composition of that deficit has changed. So that is his number one goal. We want the outcomes to make sense. We want to have more auto manufacturing here, and we are seeing it. Joe, one of the reasons why the Canadians imposed their measures is because we have seen production lines come from Canada back to the U.S. We are seeing Toyota and others move from Mexico to the U.S. That is what we want. So that is the outcome that he wants. I think also, if we can have an arrangement with Mexico and with Canada that emphasizes Canadian, Mexican, and U.S. content in goods traded in North America, that is a good outcome because that helps get supply chains back here in North America. It sometimes seems like the whole world—we could talk about the EU and this loan to Airbus. It is not just that President Trump feels like we have been harmed in this case; Boeing is asking the U.S. to intervene. What can you tell us about that? So we have had for a few years an understanding with the European Union that neither side would have unfair subsidies. There was a World Trade Organization dispute about this, where the United States won in a big way. The EU themselves had a case against us with Boeing, which was a much smaller case. But the reality is we have had a settlement with the EU for many years. However, this is coming on the heels of that settlement. The EU has announced this loan; we are looking at it very closely. I have made representations to my counterpart in the European Commission. We are speaking with Boeing about this. We do not mind fair competition; we welcome fair competition. That being said, we cannot accept a situation where companies do not have to operate under market-based systems, where Boeing has to operate and compete against an Airbus that gets unfair loans. With respect to everything that we are trying to do in America, we want to make sure that our companies can compete on a level playing field. If the European Union is giving unfair loans, then we need to take care of it and address it. Switching gears, the latest is that there are AI advances being made in China that are surprising, bringing that country up to par with the United States or even ahead in certain areas. Do we need to do things in terms of new trade actions there? Where does it stand? We have not heard much lately about proposed remedies for things like this. So this is an emergent issue. Where China is going with artificial intelligence, they are clearly our key competitor in the world on this issue. This is something that a number of U.S. government agencies are looking at and collaborating on, whether it is Mike Kratsios in the Office of Science and Technology Policy in the White House, the Commerce Department, the Treasury Department, or the War Department. We look at it. We are concerned when we see allegations that China is distilling our models to use theirs. We know that this is something that is going on. We see that as a form of forced technology transfer or IP theft, in a sense. We also know that they subsidize energy production that is going into their AI. We know that they are getting an unfair advantage in a lot of these ways. They are promoting certain types of open AI standards that make it actually really cheap for others to use them. And I think because we are in this emergent situation, I do not think we are necessarily against one type of standard or another, but I think we are taking a very close look at how China is propagating its AI development to make sure that our companies compete again on a level playing field. Ambassador, could you just clarify how you think our relationship with Canada stands right now, particularly with the USMCA not being renewed for 16 years? Where would you say the state of things is between the two nations right now? Well, first of all, one thing to remember is we have always had trade issues with Canada. It has always been the most contentious part of our relationship across decades, whether it is with softwood lumber or 20 or 30 years ago with trade in grains and pork across the borders. So it is not new to have trade tensions with Canada. I would say going forward, I am going to keep talking to my counterparts in Canada. The Prime Minister put out a letter yesterday responding to the President's action. His letter was firm but moderate in tone, so they have not cut off talks. We are going to keep discussing with them. The reality is we want outcomes that are good for American workers and American businesses, and we are going to pursue those. Sometimes you have to take trade action to incentivize those actions, and sometimes you take an action that leads to a better negotiation. In the end, we will see where it leads. We will see where it leads, but do you think that if we have always had these troubles, this is not anything to get worried about? I just ask because we had the CFO of GM on earlier today. Obviously, a company like GM, which manufactures a lot of its trucks in Canada and brings them back, has a lot riding on this. There are countless other companies that are watching and waiting and thinking the same thing. Should they rest easy on that front? I think, well, let us just go back to what the President regularly says: If you are building in America, that is where you are going to have certainty. You are going to have access to the robust American consumer. Again, that is a big certainty there. I think if you are planning on building a new factory in Canada and exporting back to the United States, I think you have to understand that the American people, they do not want offshoring. This is a bipartisan view. We do not want a situation where those jobs are going north. Remember, Canada did not used to make automobiles; it was in the 60s. Canada essentially limited the import of U.S. vehicles unless our auto companies agreed to produce their own in the U.S. Canada-Auto Pact. So they did a very Trumpian thing by saying, "If you are going to sell here, you need to make it here." So we have a long history again of these tensions. I do not think that there is ever going to be a situation where if a company is offshoring to another country, they are always going to have a 100% guarantee that they will be able to get into the United States. That is different from when we had NAFTA. Starting with NAFTA, we thought it was okay to move not just fully completed vehicles, but parts back and forth across the border for the automakers. The U.S. automakers in particular, they set up a lot of things that were kind of based on that certainty. The new world is that you should not feel that comfortable if it is not actually being completely manufactured here in the United States. We fundamentally disagree on NAFTA. The President disagreed with it, and I disagreed with it. We had the UAW who did not love NAFTA. We had people on the right and left who did not agree with NAFTA. We had people running for president in the 90s against NAFTA. So that is part of the whole problem is that we do not like that certainty of saying, "You are going to go overseas, build a factory, and re-export it here." Now listen, we are always going to trade. Are there more tariffs coming? Does the FTC report provide some more detail this week? So with forced labor, the Section 301 investigation of forced labor—again, the U.S. has laws to prohibit trade in goods with forced labor. Other countries, most do not have a law. Those that do, do not really enforce it. We expect to see some action soon. I cannot really specify a timeline right now, I have not revealed that kind of thing, but we do expect action soon on that. Ambassador, the FTC reports that this would be much broader than just a small number of countries, that this would be a significant expansion. Is that not right? Well, it covers 60 trade partners, and that actually illustrates the scale of the problem we are facing. For years, again, the United States has had on the books this law where we prohibit the import of goods made in whole or in part with forced labor, and we enforce it rigorously. Other countries, most do not have it. Those that do, do not enforce it. The EU will say, "We have a law on forced labor," and while that is true, it does not come into effect for another year and a half. So, by the terms of their own law, it is not in effect. It does cover 60 countries because it is that widespread.
